<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Early human migrations</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Spread of humans from Africa through the world</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_human_migration" title="History of human migration">History of human migration</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pre-modern_human_migration" title="Pre-modern human migration">Pre-modern human migration</a></div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Putative_migration_waves_out_of_Africa.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1002" data-file-width="1210" decoding="async" height="290" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Putative_migration_waves_out_of_Africa.png/350px-Putative_migration_waves_out_of_Africa.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Putative_migration_waves_out_of_Africa.png/525px-Putative_migration_waves_out_of_Africa.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Putative_migration_waves_out_of_Africa.png/700px-Putative_migration_waves_out_of_Africa.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Putative_migration_waves_out_of_Africa.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Putative migration waves <a href="/wiki/Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Recent African origin of modern humans">out of Africa</a> and back migrations into the continent, as well as the locations of major ancient human remains and archeological sites (López et al.2015).</div></div></div>
<p><b>Early human migrations</b> are the earliest <a href="/wiki/Human_migration" title="Human migration">migrations and expansions</a> of <a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">archaic and modern humans</a> across continents.  They are believed to have begun approximately 2 million years ago with the <a href="/wiki/Early_expansions_of_hominins_out_of_Africa" title="Early expansions of hominins out of Africa">early expansions out of Africa</a> by <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i>. This initial migration was followed by other <a href="/wiki/Archaic_humans" title="Archaic humans">archaic humans</a> including <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_heidelbergensis" title="Homo heidelbergensis">H. heidelbergensis</a></i>, which lived around 500,000 years ago and was the likely ancestor of <a href="/wiki/Denisovan" title="Denisovan">Denisovans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Neanderthals</a> as well as modern humans. Early hominids likely "crossed land bridges that were eventually covered in water" (History Alive, pub. 2004, TCI<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed. (August 2022)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>).
</p><p>Within Africa, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i> dispersed around the time of its <a href="/wiki/Speciation" title="Speciation">speciation</a>, roughly 300,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-350kiloyearsAgo_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-350kiloyearsAgo-3">[note 1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20190710b_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20190710b-4">[3]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Recent African origin of modern humans">recent African origin</a> paradigm suggests that the anatomically modern humans outside of Africa descend from a population of <i>Homo sapiens</i> migrating from <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a> roughly 70–50,000 years ago and spreading <a href="/wiki/Southern_Dispersal" title="Southern Dispersal">along the southern coast</a> of Asia and to Oceania by about 50,000 years ago. Modern humans spread <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_early_modern_humans" title="European early modern humans">across Europe</a> about 40,000 years ago.
</p><p>Early Eurasian <i>Homo sapiens</i> fossils have been found in Israel and Greece, dated to 194,000–177,000 and 210,000 years old respectively. These fossils seem to represent failed dispersal attempts by early <i>Homo sapiens</i>, who were likely replaced by local Neanderthal populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20190710_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20190710-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PHYS-20190710_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-PHYS-20190710-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20190710a_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20190710a-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20190710b_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20190710b-4">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>The migrating modern human populations are known to have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">interbred</a> with earlier local populations, so that contemporary human populations are descended in small part (below 10% contribution) from regional varieties of archaic humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[note 2]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">Last Glacial Maximum</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_North_Eurasians" title="Ancient North Eurasians">North Eurasian</a> populations migrated <a href="/wiki/Settlement_of_the_Americas" title="Settlement of the Americas">to the Americas</a> about 20,000 years ago. Northern Eurasia was peopled after 12,000 years ago, in the beginning <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a>. Arctic Canada and Greenland were reached by the <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Eskimo" title="Paleo-Eskimo">Paleo-Eskimo</a> expansion around 4,000 years ago. Finally, <a href="/wiki/Polynesia" title="Polynesia">Polynesia</a> was populated within the past 2,000 years in the last wave of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austronesian_expansion" title="Austronesian expansion">Austronesian expansion</a>.
</p>

<h2><span id="Early_humans_.28before_Homo_sapiens.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_humans_(before_Homo_sapiens)">Early humans (before <i>Homo sapiens</i>)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early humans (before Homo sapiens)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Early_expansions_of_hominins_out_of_Africa" title="Early expansions of hominins out of Africa">Early expansions of hominins out of Africa</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">earliest humans</a> developed out of <a href="/wiki/Australopithecine" title="Australopithecine">australopithecine</a> ancestors about 3 million years ago, most likely in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Africa" title="Eastern Africa">Eastern Africa</a>, most likely in the area of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kenyan_Rift_Valley" title="Kenyan Rift Valley">Kenyan Rift Valley</a>, where the <a href="/wiki/Lomekwi" title="Lomekwi">oldest known stone tools</a> were found. Stone tools recently discovered at the <a href="/wiki/Shangchen" title="Shangchen">Shangchen</a> site in China and dated to 2.12 million years ago are claimed to be the earliest known evidence of hominins outside Africa, surpassing <a href="/wiki/Dmanisi" title="Dmanisi">Dmanisi</a> in Georgia by 300,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Homo_erectus"><i>Homo erectus</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Homo erectus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oldowan" title="Oldowan">Oldowan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Homo_lineage_2017update.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="498" data-file-width="675" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Homo_lineage_2017update.svg/300px-Homo_lineage_2017update.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Homo_lineage_2017update.svg/450px-Homo_lineage_2017update.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Homo_lineage_2017update.svg/600px-Homo_lineage_2017update.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Homo_lineage_2017update.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A model of the evolution of the genus <i>Homo</i> over the last 2 million years (vertical axis)</div></div></div>
<p>Between 2 and less than a million years ago, <i><a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">Homo</a></i> spread throughout East Africa and to <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern Africa</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Homo_ergaster" title="Homo ergaster">Homo ergaster</a></i>), but not yet to West Africa. Around 1.8 million years ago, <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Out_of_Africa_I" title="Out of Africa I">migrated out of Africa</a> via the <a href="/wiki/Levantine_corridor" title="Levantine corridor">Levantine corridor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> to <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a>. This migration has been proposed as being related to the operation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahara_Pump_Theory" title="Sahara Pump Theory">Saharan pump</a>, around 1.9 million years ago.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <i>Homo erectus</i> dispersed throughout most of the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a>, reaching as far as <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>. Its distribution is traced by the <a href="/wiki/Oldowan" title="Oldowan">Oldowan</a> lithic industry, by 1.3 million years ago extending as far north as the <a href="/wiki/40th_parallel_north" title="40th parallel north">40th parallel</a> (<a href="/wiki/Xiaochangliang" title="Xiaochangliang">Xiaochangliang</a>).
</p><p>Key sites for this early migration out of Africa are <a href="/wiki/Riwat" title="Riwat">Riwat</a> in Pakistan (~2 Ma?<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[12]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Ubeidiya_prehistoric_site" title="Ubeidiya prehistoric site">Ubeidiya</a> in the Levant (1.5 Ma) and <a href="/wiki/Dmanisi" title="Dmanisi">Dmanisi</a> in the Caucasus (1.81 ± 0.03 Ma, <a href="/wiki/P-value" title="P-value">p</a>=0.05<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[13]</a></sup>).
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehistoric_China" title="Prehistoric China">China</a> shows evidence of Erectus from 2.12 Mya in Gongwangling in Lantian county.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi.org_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-doi.org-16">[14]</a></sup> Two <i>Homo erectus</i> incisors have been found near Yuanmou, south China, and are dated to 1.7 Mya, and a cranium from Lantian has been dated to 1.63 Ma. Artefacts from Majuangou III and Shangshazui in the <a href="/wiki/Xiaochangliang" title="Xiaochangliang">Nihewan basin</a>, north China, have been dated to 1.6–1.7 Ma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi.org_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-doi.org-16">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[15]</a></sup> The archaeological site of <a href="/wiki/Xihoudu" title="Xihoudu">Xihoudu</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">西侯渡</span></span>) in <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a> Province is the earliest recorded <a href="/wiki/Control_of_fire_by_early_humans" title="Control of fire by early humans">use of fire</a> by <i>Homo erectus</i>, which is dated 1.27 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MagnetostratigraphicDating_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-MagnetostratigraphicDating-18">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>) was reached about 1.7 million years ago (<a href="/wiki/Meganthropus" title="Meganthropus">Meganthropus</a>). Western <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic_Europe" title="Paleolithic Europe">Europe</a> was first populated around 1.2 million years ago (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaeological_Site_of_Atapuerca" title="Archaeological Site of Atapuerca">Atapuerca</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Robert_G._Bednarik" title="Robert G. Bednarik">Robert G. Bednarik</a> has suggested that <i>Homo erectus</i> may have built rafts and sailed oceans, a theory that has raised some controversy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_H._erectus">After <i>H. erectus</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: After H. erectus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal_extinction" title="Neanderthal extinction">Neanderthal extinction</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Clactonian" title="Clactonian">Clactonian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Micoquien" title="Micoquien">Micoquien</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mousterian" title="Mousterian">Mousterian</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spread_and_evolution_of_Denisovans.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Spread_and_evolution_of_Denisovans.jpg/220px-Spread_and_evolution_of_Denisovans.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Spread_and_evolution_of_Denisovans.jpg/330px-Spread_and_evolution_of_Denisovans.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Spread_and_evolution_of_Denisovans.jpg/440px-Spread_and_evolution_of_Denisovans.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spread_and_evolution_of_Denisovans.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Spread of Denisovans and Neanderthals after 500,000 years ago</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Range_of_NeanderthalsAColoured.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="463" data-file-width="970" decoding="async" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Range_of_NeanderthalsAColoured.png/220px-Range_of_NeanderthalsAColoured.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Range_of_NeanderthalsAColoured.png/330px-Range_of_NeanderthalsAColoured.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Range_of_NeanderthalsAColoured.png/440px-Range_of_NeanderthalsAColoured.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Range_of_NeanderthalsAColoured.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Known Neanderthal range with separate populations in Europe and the Caucasus (blue), the Near East (orange), Uzbekistan (green), and the Altai region (purple)</div></div></div>
<p>One million years after its dispersal, <i>H. erectus</i> was diverging into new species. <i>H. erectus</i> is a <a href="/wiki/Chronospecies" title="Chronospecies">chronospecies</a> and was never extinct, so its "late survival" is a matter of taxonomic convention. Late forms of <i>H. erectus</i> are thought to have survived until after about 0.5 million ago to 143,000 years ago at the latest,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[note 3]</a></sup> with derived forms classified as <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_antecessor" title="Homo antecessor">H. antecessor</a></i> in Europe around 800,000 years ago and <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_heidelbergensis" title="Homo heidelbergensis">H. heidelbergensis</a></i> in Africa around 600,000 years ago. <i>H. heidelbergensis</i> in its turn spread across East Africa (<i><a href="/wiki/Homo_rhodesiensis" title="Homo rhodesiensis">H. rhodesiensis</a></i>) and to Eurasia, where it gave rise to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neanderthals" title="Neanderthals">Neanderthals</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Denisovans" title="Denisovans">Denisovans</a>.
</p><p><i>H. heidelbergensis</i>, Neanderthals and Denisovans expanded north beyond the <a href="/wiki/50th_parallel_north" title="50th parallel north">50th parallel</a> (<a href="/wiki/Eartham_Pit,_Boxgrove" title="Eartham Pit, Boxgrove">Eartham Pit, Boxgrove</a> 500kya, <a href="/wiki/Swanscombe_Heritage_Park" title="Swanscombe Heritage Park">Swanscombe Heritage Park</a> 400kya, <a href="/wiki/Denisova_Cave" title="Denisova Cave">Denisova Cave</a> 50 kya). It has been suggested that late Neanderthals may even have reached the boundary of the <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a>, by c. 32,000 years ago, when they were being displaced from their earlier habitats by <i>H. sapiens</i>, based on 2011 excavations at the site of Byzovaya in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urals" title="Urals">Urals</a> (<a href="/wiki/Komi_Republic" title="Komi Republic">Komi Republic</a>, <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1073938472">.mw-parser-output .geo-default,.mw-parser-output .geo-dms,.mw-parser-output .geo-dec{display:inline}.mw-parser-output .geo-nondefault,.mw-parser-output .geo-multi-punct{display:none}.mw-parser-output .longitude,.mw-parser-output .latitude{white-space:nowrap}</style><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Early_human_migrations&amp;params=65.02_N_57.42_E_"><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">65°01′N</span> <span class="longitude">57°25′E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">65.02°N 57.42°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">65.02; 57.42</span></span></span></a></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other archaic human species are assumed to have spread throughout Africa by this time, although the fossil record is sparse. Their presence is assumed based on traces of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">admixture</a> with modern humans found in the genome of African populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hamgenev_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-hamgenev-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lac12adaafr_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-lac12adaafr-26">[23]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_naledi" title="Homo naledi">Homo naledi</a></i>, discovered in South Africa in 2013 and tentatively dated to about 300,000 years ago, may represent fossil evidence of such an archaic human species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Neanderthals spread across the Near East and Europe, while Denisovans appear to have spread across Central and East Asia and to Southeast Asia and Oceania. There is evidence that Denisovans interbred with Neanderthals in Central Asia where their habitats overlapped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lopezvan_Dorp2016_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lopezvan_Dorp2016-28">[25]</a></sup> Neanderthal evidence has also been found quite late at 33,000 years ago at the 65th latitude of the Byzovaya site in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_human_settlement_in_the_Ural_Mountains#Prehistory" title="History of human settlement in the Ural Mountains">Ural Mountains</a>. This is far outside of any otherwise known habitat, during a high ice cover period, and perhaps reflects a refugia of near extinction.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Homo_sapiens"><i>Homo sapiens</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Homo sapiens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dispersal_throughout_Africa">Dispersal throughout Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Dispersal throughout Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Macro-haplogroup_L_(mtDNA)" title="Macro-haplogroup L (mtDNA)">Macro-haplogroup L (mtDNA)</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaeogenetics_of_sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Archaeogenetics of sub-Saharan Africa">Archaeogenetics of sub-Saharan Africa</a></div>
<p><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i> are believed to have emerged in Africa about 300,000 years ago, based in part on thermoluminescence dating of artefacts and remains from <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Irhoud" title="Jebel Irhoud">Jebel Irhoud</a>, Morocco, published in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[note 4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[27]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Florisbad_Skull" title="Florisbad Skull">Florisbad Skull</a> from Florisbad, South Africa, dated to about 259,000 years ago, has also been classified as early <i>Homo sapiens</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guardian_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-33">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HublinBen-Ncer2017_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-HublinBen-Ncer2017-34">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[31]</a></sup> Previously, the <a href="/wiki/Omo_remains" title="Omo remains">Omo remains</a>, excavated between 1967 and 1974 in <a href="/wiki/Omo_National_Park" title="Omo National Park">Omo National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, and dated to 200,000 years ago, were long held to be the oldest known fossils of <i>Homo sapiens</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mcdougall2005_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mcdougall2005-36">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2019, scientists reported the computerized determination, based on 260 <a href="/wiki/CT_scan" title="CT scan">CT scans</a>, of a virtual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_skull" title="Human skull">skull shape</a> of the last common human ancestor to anatomically modern humans, representative of the earliest modern humans, and suggested that modern humans arose between 260,000 and 350,000 years ago through a merging of populations in <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20190910_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20190910-37">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20190910_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20190910-38">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 2019, anthropologists reported the discovery of 210,000 year old remains of a <i>H. sapiens</i> and 170,000 year old remains of a <i>H. neanderthalensis</i> in <a href="/wiki/Apidima_Cave" title="Apidima Cave">Apidima Cave</a> in southern <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, more than 150,000 years older than previous <i>H. sapiens</i> finds in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20190710_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20190710-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PHYS-20190710_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-PHYS-20190710-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20190710a_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20190710a-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20190710b_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20190710b-4">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Early modern humans expanded to Western Eurasia and Central, Western and Southern Africa from the time of their emergence. While <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Dispersal" title="Northern Dispersal">early expansions</a> to Eurasia appear not to have persisted,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hershkovitz2018_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hershkovitz2018-39">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lopezvan_Dorp2016_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lopezvan_Dorp2016-28">[25]</a></sup> expansions to <a href="/wiki/Khoisan#Origins" title="Khoisan">Southern</a> and <a href="/wiki/African_Pygmies#Origins" title="African Pygmies">Central Africa</a> resulted in the deepest temporal divergence in living human populations. Early modern human expansion in sub-Saharan Africa appears to have contributed to the end of late <a href="/wiki/Acheulean" title="Acheulean">Acheulean</a> (<a href="/wiki/Fauresmith_(industry)" title="Fauresmith (industry)">Fauresmith</a>) industries at about 130,000 years ago, although very late coexistence of archaic and early modern humans, until as late as 12,000 years ago, has been argued for West Africa in particular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scerri2017_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scerri2017-40">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ancestors of the modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khoi-San" title="Khoi-San">Khoi-San</a> expanded to Southern Africa before 150,000 years ago, possibly as early as before 260,000 years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[note 5]</a></sup> so that by the beginning of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIS_5" title="MIS 5">MIS 5</a> "<a href="/wiki/Megadrought" title="Megadrought">megadrought</a>", 130,000 years ago, there were two ancestral population clusters in Africa, bearers of <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_L0_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup L0 (mtDNA)">mt-DNA haplogroup L0</a> in southern Africa, ancestral to the Khoi-San, and bearers of <a href="/wiki/Macro-haplogroup_L_(mtDNA)" title="Macro-haplogroup L (mtDNA)">haplogroup L1-6</a> in central/eastern Africa, ancestral to everyone else. There was a significant back-migration of bearers of L0 towards eastern Africa between 120 and 75 kya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[note 6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Expansion to Central Africa by the ancestors of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_African_forager" title="Central African forager">Central African forager</a> populations (African Pygmies) most likely took place before 130,000 years ago, and certainly before 60,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[note 7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The situation in <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a> is difficult to interpret due to a sparsity of fossil evidence. <i>Homo sapiens</i> seems to have reached the western <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahelian zone</a> by 130,000 years ago, while tropical West African sites associated with <i>H. sapiens</i> are known only from after 130,000 years ago. Unlike elsewhere in Africa, archaic <a href="/wiki/Middle_Stone_Age" title="Middle Stone Age">Middle Stone Age</a> sites appear to persist until very late, down to the Holocene boundary (12,000 years ago), pointing to the possibility of late survival of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_human" title="Archaic human">archaic humans</a>, and late <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_admixture_to_anatomically_modern_humans" title="Archaic admixture to anatomically modern humans">hybridization</a> with <i>H. sapiens</i> in West Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scerri2017_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Scerri2017-40">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_northern_Africa_dispersal">Early northern Africa dispersal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early northern Africa dispersal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Dispersal" title="Northern Dispersal">Northern Dispersal</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sahara_pump_theory" title="Sahara pump theory">Sahara pump theory</a></div>
<p>Populations of <i>Homo sapiens</i> migrated to the Levant and to Europe between 130,000 and 115,000 years ago, and possibly in earlier waves as early as 185,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[note 8]</a></sup>
</p><p>A fragment of a jawbone with eight teeth found at <a href="/wiki/Archaeology_of_Israel#Carmel_Caves" title="Archaeology of Israel">Misliya Cave</a> has been dated to around 185,000 years ago. Layers dating from between 250,000 and 140,000 years ago in the same cave contained tools of the <a href="/wiki/Levallois_technique" title="Levallois technique">Levallois</a> type which could put the date of the first migration even earlier if the tools can be associated with the modern human jawbone finds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-51">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-52">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>These early migrations do not appear to have led to lasting colonisation and receded by about 80,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lopezvan_Dorp2016_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lopezvan_Dorp2016-28">[25]</a></sup> There is a possibility that this first wave of expansion may have reached China (or even North America<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (January 2018)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> – <a href="/wiki/Talk:Early_human_migrations#Dubious" title="Talk:Early human migrations">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[45]</a></sup>) as early as 125,000 years ago, but would have died out without leaving a trace in the genome of contemporary humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lopezvan_Dorp2016_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lopezvan_Dorp2016-28">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is some evidence that modern humans left Africa at least 125,000 years ago using two different routes: through the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile Valley</a> heading to the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, at least into modern Israel(<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qafzeh" title="Qafzeh">Qafzeh</a>: 120,000–100,000 years ago); and a second route through the present-day <a href="/wiki/Bab-el-Mandeb" title="Bab-el-Mandeb">Bab-el-Mandeb</a> Strait on the Red Sea (at that time, with a much lower sea level and narrower extension), crossing to the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[47]</a></sup> and settling in places like the present-day United Arab Emirates (125,000 years ago)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[48]</a></sup> and Oman (106,000 years ago),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[49]</a></sup> and possibly reaching the Indian Subcontinent (<a href="/wiki/Jwalapuram" title="Jwalapuram">Jwalapuram</a>: 75,000 years ago.) Although no human remains have yet been found in these three places, the apparent similarities between the stone tools found at <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Faya" title="Jebel Faya">Jebel Faya</a>, those from Jwalapuram and some from Africa suggest that their creators were all modern humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[50]</a></sup> These findings might give some support to the claim that modern humans from Africa arrived at southern China about 100,000 years ago (<a href="/wiki/Zhiren_Cave" title="Zhiren Cave">Zhiren Cave</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhirendong" title="Zhirendong">Zhirendong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chongzuo" title="Chongzuo">Chongzuo</a> City: 100,000 years ago;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[note 9]</a></sup> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liujiang_hominid" title="Liujiang hominid">Liujiang hominid</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liujiang_County" title="Liujiang County">Liujiang County</a>): controversially dated at 139,000–111,000 years ago <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[55]</a></sup>). Dating results of the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lunadong&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lunadong (page does not exist)">Lunadong</a> (<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bubing_Basin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bubing Basin (page does not exist)">Bubing Basin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guangxi" title="Guangxi">Guangxi</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_China" title="Southern China">southern China</a>) teeth, which include a right upper second molar and a left lower second molar, indicate that the molars may be as old as 126,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[57]</a></sup> Since these previous exits from Africa did not leave traces in the results of genetic analyses based on the Y chromosome and on MtDNA (which represent only a small part of the human genetic material), it seems that those modern humans did not survive in large numbers and were assimilated by our major antecessors. An explanation for their extinction (or small genetic imprint) may be the <a href="/wiki/Toba_catastrophe_theory" title="Toba catastrophe theory">Toba eruption</a> (74,000 years ago), though some argue it scarcely impacted human population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coastal_migration">Coastal migration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Coastal migration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Southern_Dispersal" title="Southern Dispersal">Southern Dispersal</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Recent African origin of modern humans">Recent African origin of modern humans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interbreeding_between_archaic_and_modern_humans" title="Interbreeding between archaic and modern humans">Interbreeding between archaic and modern humans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Archaic_humans_in_Southeast_Asia#Anatomically_modern_humans_in_southeast_Asia" title="Archaic humans in Southeast Asia">Archaic humans in Southeast Asia § Anatomically modern humans in southeast Asia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Early_migrations_mercator.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="569" data-file-width="1173" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Early_migrations_mercator.svg/350px-Early_migrations_mercator.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Early_migrations_mercator.svg/525px-Early_migrations_mercator.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Early_migrations_mercator.svg/700px-Early_migrations_mercator.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Early_migrations_mercator.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Overview map of the peopling of the world by early humans during the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a>, following to the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Dispersal" title="Southern Dispersal">Southern Dispersal</a> paradigm.</div></div></div>
<p>The so-called "<a href="/wiki/Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans#Southern_Route_dispersal" title="Recent African origin of modern humans">recent dispersal</a>" of modern humans took place about 70–50,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PosthC_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-PosthC-68">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaberM_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-HaberM-70">[61]</a></sup> It is this migration wave that led to the lasting spread of modern humans throughout the world.
</p><p>A small group from a population in East Africa, bearing <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_L3_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup L3 (mtDNA)">mitochondrial haplogroup L3</a> and numbering possibly fewer than 1,000 individuals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[63]</a></sup> crossed the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> strait at <a href="/wiki/Bab-el-Mandeb" title="Bab-el-Mandeb">Bab-el-Mandeb</a>, to what is now <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>, after around 75,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[64]</a></sup> A recent review has also shown support for the northern route through Sinai/Israel/Syria (Levant).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lopezvan_Dorp2016_28-4"><a href="#cite_note-Lopezvan_Dorp2016-28">[25]</a></sup> Their descendants spread along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coastal_migration" title="Coastal migration">coastal route</a> around <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">Arabia</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a> to the <a href="/wiki/South_Asian_Stone_Age" title="South Asian Stone Age">Indian subcontinent</a> before 55,000 years ago. Other research supports a migration out of Africa between about 65,000 and 50,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PosthC_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-PosthC-68">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VaiS_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-VaiS-74">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaberM_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-HaberM-70">[61]</a></sup> The coastal migration between roughly 70,000 and 50,000 years ago is associated with mitochondrial haplogroups <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_M_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup M (mtDNA)">M</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_N_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup N (mtDNA)">N</a>, both derivative of L3.
</p><p>Along the way <i>H. sapiens</i> interbred with Neanderthals and Denisovans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[66]</a></sup> with Denisovan DNA making 0.2% of mainland Asian and Native American DNA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-2013_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-2013-76">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nearby_Oceania">Nearby Oceania</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Nearby Oceania">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Migrations continued along the Asian coast to Southeast Asia and Oceania, colonising <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Australia" title="Prehistoric Australia">Australia</a> by around 65–50,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowler_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowler-77">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_2017_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wood_2017-78">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[70]</a></sup> By reaching Australia, <i>H. sapiens</i> for the first time expanded its habitat beyond that of <i>H. erectus</i>. Denisovan ancestry is shared by <a href="/wiki/Melanesians" title="Melanesians">Melanesians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aboriginal_Australians" title="Aboriginal Australians">Aboriginal Australians</a>, and smaller scattered groups of people in Southeast Asia, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mamanwa" title="Mamanwa">Mamanwa</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Negrito" title="Negrito">Negrito</a> people in the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, suggesting the interbreeding took place in Eastern Asia where the Denisovans lived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Callaway._80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Callaway.-80">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reich_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reich-81">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Choi._82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Choi.-82">[73]</a></sup> Denisovans may have crossed the <a href="/wiki/Wallace_Line" title="Wallace Line">Wallace Line</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Wallacea" title="Wallacea">Wallacea</a> serving as their last <a href="/wiki/Refugium_(population_biology)" title="Refugium (population biology)">refugium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooper_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cooper-83">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[75]</a></sup> <i>Homo erectus</i> had crossed the Lombok gap reaching as far as Flores, but never made it to Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Sunda_and_Sahul.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1007" data-file-width="1111" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Map_of_Sunda_and_Sahul.png/250px-Map_of_Sunda_and_Sahul.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Map_of_Sunda_and_Sahul.png/375px-Map_of_Sunda_and_Sahul.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Map_of_Sunda_and_Sahul.png/500px-Map_of_Sunda_and_Sahul.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Sunda_and_Sahul.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The map shows the probable extent of land and water at the time of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_glacial_maximum" title="Last glacial maximum">last glacial maximum</a>, 20,000 yrs ago and when the sea level was probably more than 110m lower than today.</div></div></div>
<p>During this time sea level was much lower and most of <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Southeast_Asia" title="Maritime Southeast Asia">Maritime Southeast Asia</a> formed one land mass known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunda_shelf" title="Sunda shelf">Sunda</a>. Migration continued Southeast on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Coastal_Migration" title="Great Coastal Migration">coastal route</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Strait" title="Strait">straits</a> between Sunda and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahul_continent" title="Sahul continent">Sahul</a>, the continental land mass of present-day Australia and <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>. The gaps on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weber_Line" title="Weber Line">Weber Line</a> are up to 90 km wide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[77]</a></sup> so the migration to Australia and New Guinea would have required seafaring skills. Migration also continued along the coast eventually turning northeast to <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and finally reaching <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> before turning inland. This is evidenced by the pattern of <a href="/wiki/Human_mitochondrial_DNA_haplogroup" title="Human mitochondrial DNA haplogroup">mitochondrial haplogroups</a> descended from <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_M_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup M (mtDNA)">haplogroup M</a>, and in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_Y-chromosome_DNA_haplogroups" title="Human Y-chromosome DNA haplogroups">Y-chromosome</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haplogroup_C_(Y-DNA)" title="Haplogroup C (Y-DNA)">haplogroup C</a>.
</p><p>Sequencing of one Aboriginal genome from an old hair sample in <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a> revealed that the individual was descended from people who migrated into East Asia between 62,000 and 75,000 years ago. This supports the theory of a single migration into Australia and New Guinea before the arrival of Modern Asians (between 25,000 and 38,000 years ago) and their later migration into North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[78]</a></sup> This migration is believed to have happened around 50,000 years ago, before Australia and New Guinea were separated by rising sea levels approximately 8,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[80]</a></sup> This is supported by a date of 50,000–60,000 years ago for the oldest evidence of settlement in Australia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowler_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bowler-77">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[81]</a></sup> around 40,000 years ago for the oldest human remains,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowler_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bowler-77">[68]</a></sup> the earliest humans artifacts which are at least 65,000 years old<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[82]</a></sup> and the extinction of the <a href="/wiki/Australian_megafauna" title="Australian megafauna">Australian megafauna</a> by humans between 46,000 and 15,000 years ago argued by Tim Flannery,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[83]</a></sup> which is similar to what happened in the Americas. The continued use of Stone Age tools in Australia has been much debated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dispersal_throughout_Eurasia">Dispersal throughout Eurasia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Dispersal throughout Eurasia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mammoth_steppe" title="Mammoth steppe">Mammoth steppe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interbreeding_between_archaic_and_modern_humans" title="Interbreeding between archaic and modern humans">Interbreeding between archaic and modern humans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mousterian" title="Mousterian">Mousterian</a></div>
<p>The population brought to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleolithic_South_Asia" title="Paleolithic South Asia">South Asia</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coastal_migration" title="Coastal migration">coastal migration</a> appears to have remained there for some time, during roughly 60,000 to 50,000 years ago, before spreading further throughout Eurasia. This dispersal of early humans, at the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a>, gave rise to the major population groups of the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Indians" title="Paleo-Indians">Americas</a>.
</p><p>Towards the West, Upper Paleolithic populations associated with mitochondrial haplogroup <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_R_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup R (mtDNA)">R</a> and its derivatives, spread throughout Asia and Europe, with a back-migration of <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_M_(mtDNA)#Haplogroup_M1" title="Haplogroup M (mtDNA)">M1</a> to North Africa and the Horn of Africa several millennia ago.
</p><p>Presence <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_early_modern_humans" title="European early modern humans">in Europe</a> is certain after 40,000 years ago, possibly as early as 43,000 years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[85]</a></sup> rapidly replacing the Neanderthal population. Contemporary Europeans have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">Neanderthal ancestry</a>, but it seems likely that substantial interbreeding with Neanderthals ceased before 47,000 years ago, i.e. took place before modern humans entered Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sank12datein_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-sank12datein-95">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is evidence from <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> that modern humans have passed through at least one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetic_bottleneck" title="Genetic bottleneck">genetic bottleneck</a>, in which genome diversity was drastically reduced. <a href="/wiki/Henry_Harpending" title="Henry Harpending">Henry Harpending</a> has proposed that humans spread from a geographically restricted area about 100,000 years ago, the passage through the geographic bottleneck and then with a dramatic growth amongst geographically dispersed populations about 50,000 years ago, beginning first in Africa and thence spreading elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harpending_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harpending-96">[87]</a></sup> Climatological and geological evidence suggests evidence for the bottleneck. The explosion of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Toba" title="Lake Toba">Toba</a>, the largest volcanic eruption of the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary" title="Quaternary">Quaternary</a>, may have created a 1,000 year cold period, potentially reducing human populations to a few tropical refugia. It has been estimated that as few as 15,000 humans survived. In such circumstances genetic drift and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Founder_effects" title="Founder effects">founder effects</a> may have been maximised. The greater diversity amongst African genomes may reflect the extent of African refugia during the Toba incident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[88]</a></sup> However, a recent review highlights that the single-source hypothesis of non-African populations is less consistent with ancient DNA analysis than multiple sources with genetic mixing across Eurasia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lopezvan_Dorp2016_28-5"><a href="#cite_note-Lopezvan_Dorp2016-28">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic_Europe" title="Paleolithic Europe">Paleolithic Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aurignacian" title="Aurignacian">Aurignacian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gravettian" title="Gravettian">Gravettian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Art_of_the_Upper_Paleolithic" title="Art of the Upper Paleolithic">Art of the Upper Paleolithic</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Hominid_dispersals_in_Europe" title="Hominid dispersals in Europe">Hominid dispersals in Europe</a></div>
<p>The recent expansion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans" title="Anatomically modern humans">anatomically modern humans</a> reached Europe around 40,000 years ago from Central Asia and the Middle East, as a result of cultural adaption to big game hunting of <a href="/wiki/Subglacial_lake" title="Subglacial lake">sub-glacial</a> steppe fauna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[89]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neanderthals" title="Neanderthals">Neanderthals</a> were present both in the Middle East and in Europe, and the arriving populations of anatomically modern humans (also known as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cro-Magnon" title="Cro-Magnon">Cro-Magnon</a>" or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_early_modern_humans" title="European early modern humans">European early modern humans</a>) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">interbred with Neanderthal populations</a> to a limited degree. Populations of modern humans and Neanderthal overlapped in various regions such as the Iberian peninsula and the Middle East. Interbreeding may have contributed Neanderthal genes to palaeolithic and ultimately modern Eurasians and Oceanians.
</p><p>An important difference between Europe and other parts of the inhabited world was the northern latitude. Archaeological evidence suggests humans, whether Neanderthal or Cro-Magnon, reached <a href="/wiki/Mamontovaya_Kurya" title="Mamontovaya Kurya">sites in Arctic Russia</a> by 40,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pavlov_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pavlov-99">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cro-Magnon are considered the first anatomically modern humans in Europe. They entered <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Zagros_Mountains" title="Zagros Mountains">Zagros Mountains</a> (near present-day <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and eastern <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>) around 50,000 years ago, with one group rapidly settling coastal areas around the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> and another migrating north to the steppes of <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genographic_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Genographic-100">[91]</a></sup> Modern human remains dating to 43–45,000 years ago have been discovered in Italy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[92]</a></sup> and Britain,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[93]</a></sup> as well as in the European Russian Arctic from 40,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pavlov_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pavlov-99">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>Humans colonised the environment west of the Urals, hunting reindeer especially,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffecker,_J_2006_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffecker,_J_2006-104">[95]</a></sup> but were faced with adaptive challenges; winter temperatures averaged from −20 to −30 °C (−4 to −22 °F) with fuel and shelter scarce. They travelled on foot and relied on hunting highly mobile herds for food. These challenges were overcome through technological innovations: tailored clothing from the pelts of fur-bearing animals; construction of shelters with hearths using bones as fuel; and digging "ice cellars" into the permafrost to store meat and bones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffecker,_J_2006_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffecker,_J_2006-104">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> sequence of two Cro-Magnons from the <a href="/wiki/Paglicci_Cave" title="Paglicci Cave">Paglicci Cave</a> in Italy, dated to 23,000 and 24,000 years old (Paglicci 52 and 12), identified the <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mtDNA</a> as <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_N_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup N (mtDNA)">Haplogroup N</a>, typical of the latter group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tnone center"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:696px;max-width:696px"><div class="trow"><div class="theader">Migration of modern humans into Europe, based on simulation by Currat &amp; Excoffier (2004)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Currat_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Currat-107">[98]</a></sup><br/>
(YBP = <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Years_before_present" title="Years before present">Years before present</a>)</div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:172px;max-width:172px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cro-Magnon_range_37,500_ybp.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="130" data-file-width="147" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Cro-Magnon_range_37%2C500_ybp.svg/170px-Cro-Magnon_range_37%2C500_ybp.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Cro-Magnon_range_37%2C500_ybp.svg/255px-Cro-Magnon_range_37%2C500_ybp.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Cro-Magnon_range_37%2C500_ybp.svg/340px-Cro-Magnon_range_37%2C500_ybp.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><b>Up to 37,500 YBP</b></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:172px;max-width:172px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cro-Magnon_range_35,000_ybp.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="130" data-file-width="147" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Cro-Magnon_range_35%2C000_ybp.svg/170px-Cro-Magnon_range_35%2C000_ybp.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Cro-Magnon_range_35%2C000_ybp.svg/255px-Cro-Magnon_range_35%2C000_ybp.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Cro-Magnon_range_35%2C000_ybp.svg/340px-Cro-Magnon_range_35%2C000_ybp.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><b>Up to 35,000 YBP</b></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:172px;max-width:172px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cro-Magnon_range_32,500_ybp.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="130" data-file-width="147" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Cro-Magnon_range_32%2C500_ybp.svg/170px-Cro-Magnon_range_32%2C500_ybp.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Cro-Magnon_range_32%2C500_ybp.svg/255px-Cro-Magnon_range_32%2C500_ybp.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Cro-Magnon_range_32%2C500_ybp.svg/340px-Cro-Magnon_range_32%2C500_ybp.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><b>Up to 32,500 YBP</b></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:172px;max-width:172px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cro-Magnon_range_30,000_ybp.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="130" data-file-width="147" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Cro-Magnon_range_30%2C000_ybp.svg/170px-Cro-Magnon_range_30%2C000_ybp.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Cro-Magnon_range_30%2C000_ybp.svg/255px-Cro-Magnon_range_30%2C000_ybp.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Cro-Magnon_range_30%2C000_ybp.svg/340px-Cro-Magnon_range_30%2C000_ybp.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><b>Up to 30,000 YBP</b></div></div></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neanderthal_extinction_hypotheses" title="Neanderthal extinction hypotheses">Neanderthal extinction hypotheses</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">Archaic human admixture with modern humans</a></div>
<p>The expansion of modern human population is thought to have begun 45,000 years ago, and it may have taken 15,000–20,000 years for Europe to be colonized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maca01_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maca01-108">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>During this time, the Neanderthals were slowly being displaced. Because it took so long for Europe to be occupied, it appears that humans and Neanderthals may have been constantly competing for territory. The Neanderthals had larger brains, and were larger overall, with a more robust or heavily built frame, which suggests that they were physically stronger than modern <i>Homo sapiens</i>. Having lived in Europe for 200,000 years, they would have been better adapted to the cold weather. The anatomically modern humans known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cro-Magnons" title="Cro-Magnons">Cro-Magnons</a>, with widespread trade networks, superior technology and bodies likely better suited to running, would eventually completely displace the Neanderthals, whose last refuge was in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" title="Iberian peninsula">Iberian peninsula</a>. After about 25,000 years ago the fossil record of the Neanderthals ends, indicating extinction. The last known population lived around a cave system on the remote south-facing coast of <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar" title="Gibraltar">Gibraltar</a> from 30,000 to 24,000 years ago.
</p><p>From the extent of linkage disequilibrium, it was estimated that the last Neanderthal gene flow into early ancestors of Europeans occurred 47,000–65,000 years <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">BP</a>. In conjunction with archaeological and fossil evidence, interbreeding is thought to have occurred somewhere in Western Eurasia, possibly the Middle East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sank12datein_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-sank12datein-95">[86]</a></sup> Studies show a higher Neanderthal admixture in East Asians than in Europeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meyer12highcov_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-meyer12highcov-110">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wall13higher_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-wall13higher-111">[102]</a></sup> North African groups share a similar excess of derived alleles with Neanderthals as non-African populations, whereas Sub-Saharan African groups are the only modern human populations with no substantial Neanderthal admixture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[note 10]</a></sup> The Neanderthal-linked haplotype B006 of the dystrophin gene has also been found among nomadic pastoralist groups in the Sahel and Horn of Africa, who are associated with northern populations. Consequently, the presence of this B006 haplotype on the northern and northeastern perimeter of Sub-Saharan Africa is attributed to gene flow from a non-African point of origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[note 11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="East.2C_Southeast_and_North_Asia"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="East,_Southeast_and_North_Asia">East, Southeast and North Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: East, Southeast and North Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Mongoloid#Genetic_research" title="Mongoloid">Mongoloid § Genetic_research</a></div>
<p>"<a href="/wiki/Tianyuan_man" title="Tianyuan man">Tianyuan man</a>", an individual who lived in China c. 40,000 years ago, showed substantial Neanderthal admixture. A 2017 study of the ancient DNA of Tianyuan Man found that the individual is related to modern Asian and Native American populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[105]</a></sup> A 2013 study found <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neanderthal_admixture" title="Neanderthal admixture">Neanderthal introgression</a> of 18 genes within the chromosome 3p21.31 region (HYAL region) of East Asians. The introgressive haplotypes were positively selected in only East Asian populations, rising steadily from 45,000 years ago until a sudden increase of growth rate around 5,000 to 3,500 years ago. They occur at very high frequencies among East Asian populations in contrast to other Eurasian populations (e.g. European and South Asian populations). The findings also suggest that this Neanderthal introgression occurred within the ancestral population shared by East Asians and Native Americans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dinch3_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-dinch3-117">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2016 study presented an analysis of the population genetics of the <a href="/wiki/Ainu_people" title="Ainu people">Ainu</a> people of northern Japan as key to the reconstruction of the early peopling of East Asia. The Ainu were found to represent a more basal branch than the modern farming populations of East Asia, suggesting an ancient (pre-Neolithic) connection with northeast Siberians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[107]</a></sup> A 2013 study associated several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenotypical" title="Phenotypical">phenotypical</a> traits associated with Mongoloids with a single mutation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EDAR" title="EDAR">EDAR</a> gene, dated to c. 35,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[note 12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[note 13]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_North_Eurasians" title="Ancient North Eurasians">Ancient North Eurasians</a></div>
<p>Mitochondrial haplogroups <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_A_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup A (mtDNA)">A</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_B_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup B (mtDNA)">B</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_G_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup G (mtDNA)">G</a> originated about 50,000 years ago, and bearers subsequently colonized <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Korea" title="Prehistoric Korea">Korea</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Japan" title="Prehistoric Japan">Japan</a>, by about 35,000 years ago. Parts of these populations migrated to North America during the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">Last Glacial Maximum</a>.
</p><p>A review paper by Melinda A. Yang (in 2022) summarized and concluded that a distinctive "Basal-East Asian population" referred to as '<i>East- and Southeast Asian lineage</i>' (ESEA); which is ancestral to modern East Asians, <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polynesians" title="Polynesians">Polynesians</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleosiberian_peoples" title="Paleosiberian peoples">Siberians</a>, originated in <a href="/wiki/Mainland_Southeast_Asia" title="Mainland Southeast Asia">Mainland Southeast Asia</a> at ~50,000BC, and expanded through multiple migration waves southwards and northwards respectively. This ESEA lineage gave rise to various sublineages, and is also ancestral to the <a href="/wiki/Hoabinhian" title="Hoabinhian">Hoabinhian hunter-gatherers</a> of Southeast Asia and the ~40,000 year old <a href="/wiki/Tianyuan_man" title="Tianyuan man">Tianyuan</a> lineage found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_China" title="Northern China">Northern China</a>, but already differentiated and distinct from <a href="/wiki/Genetic_history_of_Europe" title="Genetic history of Europe">European-related</a> and <a href="/wiki/Australasia" title="Australasia">Australasian-related</a> lineages, found in other regions of prehistoric Eurasia. The ESEA lineage trifuricated from an earlier East-Eurasian or "eastern non-African" (ENA) lineage somewhere in <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>, which also contributed to the formation of indigenous South Asian hunter-gatherers (AASI) as well as to Australasians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_Glacial_Maximum">Last Glacial Maximum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Last Glacial Maximum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Eurasia">Eurasia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Eurasia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Solutrean" title="Solutrean">Solutrean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magdalenian" title="Magdalenian">Magdalenian</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_gene_flow_in_and_out_of_Beringia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="884" data-file-width="708" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Map_of_gene_flow_in_and_out_of_Beringia.jpg/170px-Map_of_gene_flow_in_and_out_of_Beringia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Map_of_gene_flow_in_and_out_of_Beringia.jpg/255px-Map_of_gene_flow_in_and_out_of_Beringia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Map_of_gene_flow_in_and_out_of_Beringia.jpg/340px-Map_of_gene_flow_in_and_out_of_Beringia.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_gene_flow_in_and_out_of_Beringia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Schematic illustration of the <a href="/wiki/Beringia" title="Beringia">Beringia</a> migration based on <a href="/wiki/Human_mitochondrial_DNA_haplogroup" title="Human mitochondrial DNA haplogroup">matrilineal genetics</a>: Arrival of Central Asian populations to the Beringian <a href="/wiki/Mammoth_steppe" title="Mammoth steppe">Mammoth steppe</a> c. 25,000 years ago, followed by a "swift peoplling of the Americas"<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> c. 15,000 years ago.</div></div></div>
<p>Around 20,000 years ago, approximately 5,000 years after the Neanderthal extinction, the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">Last Glacial Maximum</a> forced northern hemisphere inhabitants to migrate to several <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum_refugia" title="Last Glacial Maximum refugia">shelters</a> (<a href="/wiki/Refugium_(population_biology)" title="Refugium (population biology)">refugia</a>) until the end of this period. The resulting populations are presumed to have resided in such refuges during the LGM to ultimately reoccupy Europe, where archaic historical populations are considered their descendants. The composition of European populations was later altered by further migrations, notably the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Europe" title="Neolithic Europe">Neolithic</a> expansion from the Middle East, and still later the <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a> population movements associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-European_expansion" title="Indo-European expansion">Indo-European expansion</a>. A Paleolithic site on the Yana River, Siberia, at 71°N, lies well above the Arctic Circle and dates to 27,000 radiocarbon years before present, during glacial times. This site shows that people adapted to this harsh, high-latitude, Late Pleistocene environment much earlier than previously thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Americas">Americas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Americas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Settlement_of_the_Americas" title="Settlement of the Americas">Settlement of the Americas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genetic_history_of_Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Genetic history of Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Genetic history of Indigenous peoples of the Americas</a></div>
