<p><a href="/wiki/Paleo-Indians" title="Paleo-Indians">Paleo-Indians</a> originated from <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, crossing the <a href="/wiki/Beringia" title="Beringia">Beringia land bridge</a> between eastern Siberia and present-day Alaska.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpencerWells2_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-SpencerWells2-126">[113]</a></sup> Humans lived throughout the Americas by the end of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">last glacial period</a>, or more specifically what is known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Glacial_Maximum#North_America" title="Late Glacial Maximum">late glacial maximum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpencerWells2_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-SpencerWells2-126">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smithsonian_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smithsonian-127">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[116]</a></sup> Details of Paleo-Indian migration to and throughout the American continent, including the dates and the routes traveled, are subject to ongoing research and discussion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-national_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-national-130">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Conventional estimates have it that humans reached North America at some point between 15,000 and 20,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wells2006_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wells2006-131">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Relethford2017_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Relethford2017-132">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birx2010_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Birx2010-133">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KiczaHorn2016_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-KiczaHorn2016-134">[121]</a></sup> The traditional theory is that these early migrants moved when sea levels were significantly lowered due to the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation" title="Quaternary glaciation">Quaternary glaciation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smithsonian_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-Smithsonian-127">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-national_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-national-130">[117]</a></sup> following herds of now-extinct <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">pleistocene</a> <a href="/wiki/Megafauna" title="Megafauna">megafauna</a> along <i>ice-free corridors</i> that stretched between the <a href="/wiki/Laurentide_Ice_Sheet" title="Laurentide Ice Sheet">Laurentide</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cordilleran_Ice_Sheet" title="Cordilleran Ice Sheet">Cordilleran</a> ice sheets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[122]</a></sup> Another route proposed is that, either on foot or using <a href="/wiki/Boat#History" title="Boat">primitive boats</a>, they migrated down the Pacific coast to <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> as far as <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[123]</a></sup> Any archaeological evidence of coastal occupation during the last Ice Age would now have been covered by the <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">sea level rise</a>, up to a hundred metres since then.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[124]</a></sup> The recent finding of indigenous <a href="/wiki/Australasia" title="Australasia">Australasian</a> genetic markers in Amazonia supports the coastal route hypothesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Holocene_migrations">Holocene migrations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Holocene migrations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Pre-modern_human_migration" title="Pre-modern human migration">Pre-modern human migration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_homeland" title="Linguistic homeland">Linguistic homeland</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prehistoric_migration_routes_for_Y-chromosome_Haplogroup_N_lineage_following_the_retreat_of_ice_sheets_after_the_Last_Glacial_Maximum_(22%E2%80%9318_kya).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1388" data-file-width="1885" decoding="async" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Prehistoric_migration_routes_for_Y-chromosome_Haplogroup_N_lineage_following_the_retreat_of_ice_sheets_after_the_Last_Glacial_Maximum_%2822%E2%80%9318_kya%29.jpg/330px-Prehistoric_migration_routes_for_Y-chromosome_Haplogroup_N_lineage_following_the_retreat_of_ice_sheets_after_the_Last_Glacial_Maximum_%2822%E2%80%9318_kya%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Prehistoric_migration_routes_for_Y-chromosome_Haplogroup_N_lineage_following_the_retreat_of_ice_sheets_after_the_Last_Glacial_Maximum_%2822%E2%80%9318_kya%29.jpg/495px-Prehistoric_migration_routes_for_Y-chromosome_Haplogroup_N_lineage_following_the_retreat_of_ice_sheets_after_the_Last_Glacial_Maximum_%2822%E2%80%9318_kya%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Prehistoric_migration_routes_for_Y-chromosome_Haplogroup_N_lineage_following_the_retreat_of_ice_sheets_after_the_Last_Glacial_Maximum_%2822%E2%80%9318_kya%29.jpg/660px-Prehistoric_migration_routes_for_Y-chromosome_Haplogroup_N_lineage_following_the_retreat_of_ice_sheets_after_the_Last_Glacial_Maximum_%2822%E2%80%9318_kya%29.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prehistoric_migration_routes_for_Y-chromosome_Haplogroup_N_lineage_following_the_retreat_of_ice_sheets_after_the_Last_Glacial_Maximum_(22%E2%80%9318_kya).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Prehistoric migration routes for Y-chromosome Haplogroup N lineage following the retreat of ice sheets after the Last Glacial Maximum (22–18 kya).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[127]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> is taken to begin 12,000 years ago, after the end of the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">Last Glacial Maximum</a>. During the <a href="/wiki/Holocene_climatic_optimum" title="Holocene climatic optimum">Holocene climatic optimum</a>, beginning about 9,000 years ago, human populations which had been geographically confined to <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum_refugia" title="Last Glacial Maximum refugia">refugia</a> began to migrate. By this time, most parts of the globe had been settled by <i>H. sapiens</i>; however, large areas that had been covered by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glaciers" title="Glaciers">glaciers</a> were now re-populated.
</p><p>This period sees the transition from the <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> stage throughout the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate_zone" title="Temperate zone">temperate zone</a>. The Neolithic subsequently gives way to the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> in <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a> cultures and the gradual emergence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historical_record" title="Historical record">historical record</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">Near East</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_China" title="Bronze Age China">China</a> beginning around 4,000 years ago.
</p><p>Large-scale migrations of the Mesolithic to Neolithic era are thought to have given rise to the pre-modern distribution of the world's major <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Language_families" title="Language families">language families</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Niger%E2%80%93Congo_languages" title="Niger–Congo languages">Niger-Congo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nilo-Saharan_languages" title="Nilo-Saharan languages">Nilo-Saharan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afro-Asiatic_languages" title="Afro-Asiatic languages">Afro-Asiatic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uralic_languages" title="Uralic languages">Uralic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sino-Tibetan_languages" title="Sino-Tibetan languages">Sino-Tibetan</a> or <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> phyla. The speculative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nostratic_language" title="Nostratic language">Nostratic theory</a> postulates the derivation of the major language families of Eurasia (excluding Sino-Tibetan) from a single proto-language spoken at the beginning of the Holocene period.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Eurasia_2">Eurasia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Eurasia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_migrations" title="Indo-European migrations">Indo-European migrations</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Proto-Uralic_homeland" title="Proto-Uralic homeland">Proto-Uralic homeland</a></div>
<p>Evidence published in 2014 from genome analysis of ancient human remains suggests that the modern native populations of Europe largely descend from three distinct lineages: "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Hunter-Gatherers" title="Western Hunter-Gatherers">Western Hunter-Gatherers</a>", derivative of the Cro-Magnon population of Europe, <a href="/wiki/Early_European_Farmers" title="Early European Farmers">Early European Farmers</a> introduced to Europe from the Near East during the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_North_Eurasians" title="Ancient North Eurasians">Ancient North Eurasians</a> which expanded to Europe in the context of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-European_expansion" title="Indo-European expansion">Indo-European expansion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScienceMag4Sep14_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceMag4Sep14-141">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sub-Saharan_Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Sub-Saharan Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nilo-Saharan" title="Nilo-Saharan">Nilo-Saharan</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Niger-Congo" title="Niger-Congo">Niger-Congo</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Nilotic_peoples" title="Nilotic peoples">Nilotic peoples</a> are thought to be derived from an earlier undifferentiated <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Sudanic_languages" title="Eastern Sudanic languages">Eastern Sudanic</a> unity by the 3rd millennium BCE. The development of the Proto-Nilotes as a group may have been connected with their domestication of <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a>. The Eastern Sudanic unity must have been considerably earlier still, perhaps around the 5th millennium BCE (while the proposed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nilo-Saharan" title="Nilo-Saharan">Nilo-Saharan</a> unity would date to the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a> about 15kya). The original locus of the early Nilotic speakers was presumably east of the Nile in what is now <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a>. The Proto-Nilotes of the 3rd millennium BCE were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pastoralists" title="Pastoralists">pastoralists</a>, while their neighbors, the Proto-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_Sudanic" title="Central Sudanic">Central Sudanic</a> peoples, were mostly agriculturalists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Niger-Congo" title="Niger-Congo">Niger-Congo</a> phylum is thought to have emerged around 6,000 years ago in West or Central Africa. Its expansion may have been associated with the expansion of Sahel agriculture in the African Neolithic period, following the desiccation of the Sahara in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/5.9_kiloyear_event" title="5.9 kiloyear event">c. 3900 BCE</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[130]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bantu_expansion" title="Bantu expansion">Bantu expansion</a> has spread the <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu languages</a> to Central, Eastern and Southern Africa, partly replacing the <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">indigenous</a> populations of these regions, including the <a href="/wiki/African_Pygmies" title="African Pygmies">African Pygmies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hadza_people" title="Hadza people">Hadza people</a> and <a href="/wiki/San_people" title="San people">San people</a>. Beginning about 3,000 years ago, it reached South Africa
about 1,700 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some evidence (including a 2016 study by Busby et al.) suggests admixture from ancient and recent migrations from <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> into parts of Sub-Saharan Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[132]</a></sup> Another study (Ramsay et al. 2018) also shows evidence that ancient Eurasians migrated into Africa and that Eurasian admixture in modern Sub-Saharan Africans ranges from 0% to 50%, varying by region and generally higher in the Horn of Africa and parts of the <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a> zone, and found to a lesser degree in certain parts of Western Africa, and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern Africa</a> (excluding recent immigrants).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Indo-Pacific">Indo-Pacific</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Indo-Pacific">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_peoples#Austronesian_expansion" title="Austronesian peoples">Austronesian peoples § Austronesian expansion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="425" data-file-width="1053" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific.svg/330px-Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific.svg/495px-Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific.svg/660px-Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chronological map of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austronesian_expansion" title="Austronesian expansion">Austronesian expansion</a></div></div></div>
<p>The first seaborne human migrations were by the <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_peoples" title="Austronesian peoples">Austronesian peoples</a> originating from <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> known as the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austronesian_expansion" title="Austronesian expansion">Austronesian expansion</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meacham1984_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-meacham1984-147">[134]</a></sup> Using advanced sailing technologies like <a href="/wiki/Catamaran" title="Catamaran">catamarans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Outrigger_boat" title="Outrigger boat">outrigger boats</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Crab_claw_sail" title="Crab claw sail">crab claw sails</a>, they built the first sea-going ships and rapidly colonized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Island_Southeast_Asia" title="Island Southeast Asia">Island Southeast Asia</a> at around 3000 to 1500 BCE. From the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Indonesia" title="Eastern Indonesia">Eastern Indonesia</a> they colonized <a href="/wiki/Micronesia" title="Micronesia">Micronesia</a> by 2200 to 1000 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meacham1984_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-meacham1984-147">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bellwood_1991_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bellwood_1991-148">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>A branch of the Austronesians reached <a href="/wiki/Island_Melanesia" title="Island Melanesia">Island Melanesia</a> between 1600 and 1000 BCE, establishing the <a href="/wiki/Lapita_culture" title="Lapita culture">Lapita culture</a> (named after the archaeological site in Lapita, <a href="/wiki/New_Caledonia" title="New Caledonia">New Caledonia</a>, where their characteristic pottery was first discovered). They are the direct ancestors of the modern <a href="/wiki/Polynesians" title="Polynesians">Polynesians</a>. They ventured into <a href="/wiki/Remote_Oceania" title="Remote Oceania">Remote Oceania</a> reaching <a href="/wiki/Vanuatu" title="Vanuatu">Vanuatu</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Caledonia" title="New Caledonia">New Caledonia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fiji" title="Fiji">Fiji</a> by 1200 BCE, and <a href="/wiki/Samoa" title="Samoa">Samoa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tonga" title="Tonga">Tonga</a> by around 900 to 800 BCE. This was the furthest extent of the Lapita culture expansion. During a period of around 1,500 years, they gradually lost the technology for pottery (likely due to the lack of clay deposits in the islands), replacing it with carved wooden and bamboo containers. Back-migrations from the Lapita culture also merged back Island Southeast Asia in 1500 BCE, and into Micronesia at around 200 BCE. It was not until 700 CE when they started voyaging further into the Pacific Ocean, when they colonized the <a href="/wiki/Cook_Islands" title="Cook Islands">Cook Islands</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Society_Islands" title="Society Islands">Society Islands</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marquesas" title="Marquesas">Marquesas</a>. From there, they further colonized <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a> by 900 CE, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rapa_Nui" title="Rapa Nui">Rapa Nui</a> by 1000 CE, and <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> by 1200 CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bellwood_1991_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bellwood_1991-148">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heath2017_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-heath2017-149">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carson2013_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-carson2013-150">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>, Austronesians from <a href="/wiki/Borneo" title="Borneo">Borneo</a> also colonized <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comoros_Islands" title="Comoros Islands">Comoros Islands</a> by around 500 CE. Austronesians remain the dominant ethnolinguistic group of the islands of the Indo-Pacific, and were the first to establish a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austronesian_maritime_trade_network" title="Austronesian maritime trade network">maritime trade network</a> reaching as far west as <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabian_peninsula" title="Arabian peninsula">Arabian peninsula</a>. They assimilated earlier <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> to early <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> human overland migrations through <a href="/wiki/Sundaland" title="Sundaland">Sundaland</a> like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papuans" title="Papuans">Papuans</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Negrito" title="Negrito">Negritos</a> in Island Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meacham1984_147-2"><a href="#cite_note-meacham1984-147">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bellwood_1991_148-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bellwood_1991-148">[135]</a></sup> The Austronesian expansion was the last and the most far-reaching <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> human migration event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lansing_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lansing-151">[138]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Caribbean">Caribbean</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Caribbean">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> was one of the last places in the Americas that were settled by humans. The oldest remains are known from the Greater Antilles (Cuba and Hispaniola) dating between 4000 and 3500 BCE, and comparisons between tool-technologies suggest that these peoples moved across the Yucatán Channel from Central America. All evidence suggests that later migrants from 2000 BCE and onwards originated from South America, via the Orinoco region. The descendants of these migrants include the ancestors of the <a href="/wiki/Ta%C3%ADno" title="Taíno">Taíno</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kalinago" title="Kalinago">Kalinago</a> (Island Carib) peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Arctic">Arctic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Arctic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Circumpolar_peoples" title="Circumpolar peoples">Circumpolar peoples</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dorset,_Norse,_and_Thule_cultures_900-1500.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="525" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Dorset%2C_Norse%2C_and_Thule_cultures_900-1500.svg/220px-Dorset%2C_Norse%2C_and_Thule_cultures_900-1500.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Dorset%2C_Norse%2C_and_Thule_cultures_900-1500.svg/330px-Dorset%2C_Norse%2C_and_Thule_cultures_900-1500.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Dorset%2C_Norse%2C_and_Thule_cultures_900-1500.svg/440px-Dorset%2C_Norse%2C_and_Thule_cultures_900-1500.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dorset,_Norse,_and_Thule_cultures_900-1500.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing the decline of the Paleo-Eskimo <a href="/wiki/Dorset_culture" title="Dorset culture">Dorset culture</a> and expansion of the <a href="/wiki/Thule_people" title="Thule people">Thule people</a> (900 to 1500 CE)</div></div></div>
<p>The earliest inhabitants of North America's central and eastern Arctic are referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_small_tool_tradition" title="Arctic small tool tradition">Arctic small tool tradition</a> (AST) and existed c. 2500 BCE. AST consisted of several <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Eskimo" title="Paleo-Eskimo">Paleo-Eskimo</a> cultures, including the <a href="/wiki/Independence_I_culture" title="Independence I culture">Independence cultures</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pre-Dorset" title="Pre-Dorset">Pre-Dorset</a> culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffecker_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffecker-153">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibbon_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gibbon-154">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">Inuit</a> are the descendants of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thule_culture" title="Thule culture">Thule culture</a>, which emerged from western Alaska around AD 1000 and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inuit_expansion" title="Inuit expansion">gradually displaced</a> the Dorset culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[143]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-350kiloyearsAgo-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-350kiloyearsAgo_3-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Based on Schlebusch et al., "Southern African ancient genomes estimate modern human divergence to 350,000 to 260,000 years ago",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schlebusch350-260_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schlebusch350-260-1">[1]</a></sup> <a class="external text" href="https://d2ufo47lrtsv5s.cloudfront.net/content/sci/early/2017/09/27/science.aao6266/F3.large.jpg" rel="nofollow">Fig. 3</a> (<i>H. sapiens</i> divergence times) and Stringer (2012),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stringer2012_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stringer2012-2">[2]</a></sup> (archaic admixture).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Archaic admixture from various sources is known from Europe and Asia (Neanderthals), Southeast Asia and Melanesia (Denisovans) as well as from Western and Southern Africa. The proportion of admixture varies by region, but in all cases has been reported below 10%: In Eurasian mostly estimated at 1–4% (with a high estimate of 3.4–7.3% by Lohse (2014)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup>) in Melanesians estimated at 4–6% (Reich et al. (2010)).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup> Admixture of an unknown archaic hominin in Sub-Saharan African hunter-gatherer popultations was estimated at about 2% (Hammer et al. (2011)).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hamgenev_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-hamgenev-11">[10]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_erectus_soloensis" title="Homo erectus soloensis">Homo erectus soloensis</a></i>, found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Java_(island)" title="Java (island)">Java</a>, is considered the latest known specimen of <i>H. erectus</i>. Formerly dated to as late as 50,000 to 40,000 years ago, a 2011 study pushed back the date of the extinction of <i>H. e. soloensis</i> to 143,000 years ago at the latest, more likely before 550,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Here we report the ages, determined by thermoluminescence dating, of fire-heated flint artefacts obtained from new excavations at the Middle Stone Age site of Jebel Irhoud, Morocco, which are directly associated with newly discovered remains of H. sapiens. A weighted average age places these Middle Stone Age artefacts and fossils at 315±34 thousand years ago. Support is obtained through the recalculated uranium series with electron spin resonance date of 286±32 thousand years ago for a tooth from the Irhoud 3 hominin mandible."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[26]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Estimated split times given in the source cited (in kya): Human-Neanderthal: 530–690, Deep Human [H. sapiens]: 250–360, NKSP-SKSP: 150–190, Out of Africa (OOA): 70–120.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schlebusch350-260_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schlebusch350-260-1">[1]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"By ~130 ka two distinct groups of anatomically modern humans co-existed in Africa: broadly, the ancestors of many modern-day Khoe and San populations in the south and a second central/eastern African group that includes the ancestors of most extant worldwide populations. Early modern human dispersals correlate with climate changes, particularly the tropical African "megadroughts" of MIS 5 (marine isotope stage 5, 135–75 ka) which paradoxically may have facilitated expansions in central and eastern Africa, ultimately triggering the dispersal out of Africa of people carrying haplogroup L3 ~60 ka. Two south to east migrations are discernible within haplogroup L0. One, between 120 and 75 ka, represents the first unambiguous long-range modern human dispersal detected by mtDNA and might have allowed the dispersal of several markers of modernity. A second one, within the last 20 ka signalled by L0d, may have been responsible for the spread of southern click-consonant languages to eastern Africa, contrary to the view that these eastern examples constitute relicts of an ancient, much wider distribution."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[37]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"We studied the branching history of Pygmy hunter–gatherers and agricultural populations from Africa and estimated separation times and gene flow between these populations. The model identified included the early divergence of the ancestors of Pygmy hunter–gatherers and farming populations ~60,000 years ago, followed by a split of the Pygmies' ancestors into the Western and Eastern Pygmy groups ~20,000 years ago."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[42]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Early modern human presence outside of Africa has been proposed to date back to as early as 177,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hershkovitz2018_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hershkovitz2018-39">[35]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The authors of Liu (2010) seem to accept that the individual has African recent ascentry, but with Asian archaic human admixture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[51]</a></sup> See also Dennell (2010).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[52]</a></sup> Brief comments at <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[53]</a></sup> and <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[54]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-113"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-113">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"We found that North African populations have a significant excess of derived alleles shared with Neandertals, when compared to sub-Saharan Africans. This excess is similar to that found in non-African humans, a fact that can be interpreted as a sign of Neandertal admixture. Furthermore, the Neandertal's genetic signal is higher in populations with a local, pre-Neolithic North African ancestry. Therefore, the detected ancient admixture is not due to recent Near Eastern or European migrations. Sub-Saharan populations are the only ones not affected by the admixture event with Neandertals."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quinto2012-b_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quinto2012-b-112">[103]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-115"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-115">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Of 1,420 sub-Saharan chromosomes, only one copy of B006 was observed in Ethiopia, and five in Burkina Faso, one among the Rimaibe and four among the Fulani and Tuareg, nomad-pastoralists known for having contacts with northern populations (supplementary table S1, Supplementary Material online). B006 only occurrence at the northern and northeastern outskirts of sub-Saharan Africa is thus likely to be a result of gene flow from a non-African source."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[104]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-121"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-121">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Traits affected by the mutation are sweat glands, teeth, hair thickness and breast tissue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[109]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-123"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-123">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">East Asian genetics shows a number of concentrated alleles suggestive of selection pressures. This concerns the genes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EDAR" title="EDAR">EDAR</a>, <a href="/wiki/ADH1B" title="ADH1B">ADH1B</a>, <a href="/wiki/ABCC1" title="ABCC1">ABCC1</a>, and <a href="/wiki/ALDH2" title="ALDH2">ALDH2</a> in particular. The East Asian types of ADH1B are associated with <a href="/wiki/Oryza_sativa#Continental_East_Asia" title="Oryza sativa">rice domestication</a> and would thus have arisen after the c. 11,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[110]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

ailov%2C+E&amp;rft.au=Seng%2C+JW&amp;rft.au=Evseeva%2C+I&amp;rft.au=Migliano%2C+AB&amp;rft.au=Abdullah%2C+S&amp;rft.au=Andriadze%2C+G&amp;rft.au=Primorac%2C+D&amp;rft.au=Atramentova%2C+L&amp;rft.au=Utevska%2C+O&amp;rft.au=Yepiskoposyan%2C+L&amp;rft.au=Marjanovic%2C+D&amp;rft.au=Kushniarevich%2C+A&amp;rft.au=Behar%2C+DM&amp;rft.au=Gilissen%2C+C&amp;rft.au=Vissers%2C+L&amp;rft.au=Veltman%2C+JA&amp;rft.au=Balanovska%2C+E&amp;rft.au=Derenko%2C+M&amp;rft.au=Malyarchuk%2C+B&amp;rft.au=Metspalu%2C+A&amp;rft.au=Fedorova%2C+S&amp;rft.au=Eriksson%2C+A&amp;rft.au=Manica%2C+A&amp;rft.au=Mendez%2C+FL&amp;rft.au=Karafet%2C+TM&amp;rft.au=Veeramah%2C+KR&amp;rft.au=Bradman%2C+N&amp;rft.au=Hammer%2C+MF&amp;rft.au=Osipova%2C+LP&amp;rft.au=Balanovsky%2C+O&amp;rft.au=Khusnutdinova%2C+EK&amp;rft.au=Johnsen%2C+K&amp;rft.au=Remm%2C+M&amp;rft.au=Thomas%2C+MG&amp;rft.au=Tyler-Smith%2C+C&amp;rft.au=Underhill%2C+PA&amp;rft.au=Willerslev%2C+E&amp;rft.au=Nielsen%2C+R&amp;rft.au=Metspalu%2C+M&amp;rft.au=Villems%2C+R&amp;rft.au=Kivisild%2C+T&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC4381518&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEarly+human+migrations"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-124"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-124">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFYang2022">Yang, Melinda A. (6 January 2022). <a class="external text" href="http://www.pivotscipub.com/hpgg/2/1/0001" rel="nofollow">"A genetic history of migration, diversification, and admixture in Asia"</a>. <i>Human Population Genetics and Genomics</i>. <b>2</b> (1): 1–32. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.47248%2Fhpgg2202010001" rel="nofollow">10.47248/hpgg2202010001</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISSN_(identifier)" title="ISSN (identifier)">ISSN</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/2770-5005" rel="nofollow">2770-5005</a>. <q>...In contrast, mainland East and Southeast Asians and other Pacific islanders (e.g., Austronesian speakers) are closely related to each other [9,15,16] and here denoted as belonging to an East and Southeast Asian (ESEA) lineage (Box 2). …the ESEA lineage differentiated into at least three distinct ancestries: Tianyuan ancestry which can be found 40,000-33,000 years ago in northern East Asia, ancestry found today across present-day populations of East Asia, Southeast Asia, and Siberia, but whose origins are unknown, and Hòabìnhian ancestry found 8,000-4,000 years ago in Southeast Asia, but whose origins in the Upper Paleolithic are unknown.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Human+Population+Genetics+and+Genomics&amp;rft.atitle=A+genetic+history+of+migration%2C+diversification%2C+and+admixture+in+Asia&amp;rft.volume=2&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.pages=1-32&amp;rft.date=2022-01-06&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.47248%2Fhpgg2202010001&amp;rft.issn=2770-5005&amp;rft.aulast=Yang&amp;rft.aufirst=Melinda+A.&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.pivotscipub.com%2Fhpgg%2F2%2F1%2F0001&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEarly+human+migrations"></span></span>
